<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The wax gourd genomes offer insights into the genetic diversity and ancestral cucurbit karyotype"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2019 10:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-11-14"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The botanical family Cucurbitaceae includes a variety of fruit crops with global or local economic importance. How their genomes evolve and the genetic basis of diversity remain largely unexplored. In this study, we sequence the genome of the&#160;wax gourd (Benincasa hispida), which bears giant fruit up to 80&#8201;cm in length and weighing over 20 kg. Comparative analyses of six cucurbit genomes reveal that the wax gourd genome represents the most ancestral karyotype, with the predicted ancestral genome having 15 proto-chromosomes. We also resequence 146 lines of diverse germplasm and build a variation map consisting of 16 million variations. Combining population genetics and linkage mapping, we identify a number of regions/genes potentially selected during domestication and improvement, some of which likely contribute to the large fruit size in wax gourds. Our analyses of these data help to understand genome evolution and function in cucurbits. Cucurbits fruits have diverse shapes and sizes, but their genomes evolution and genetic basis of diversity are unclear. Here, the authors show that the wax gourd genome has the most ancestral karyotype among cucurbits and identify candidate genes which&#160;contribute to large fruit size by comparative and population genomics analyses."/>

itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Zhonghua-Zhang" data-author-popup="auth-Zhonghua-Zhang" data-corresp-id="c2">Zhonghua Zhang<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a>,<a href="#Aff6">6</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Chinese Academy of Agricultural Sciences" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 0526 1937, grid.410727.7, Key Laboratory of Biology and Genetic Improvement of Horticultural Crops of the Ministry of Agriculture, Sino-Dutch Joint Laboratory of Horticultural Genomics, Institute of Vegetables and Flowers, Chinese Academy of Agricultural Sciences, Beijing, 100081, China" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Qingdao Agricultural University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0000 9526 6338, grid.412608.9, College of Horticulture, Qingdao Agricultural University, Qingdao, 266109, China" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The botanical family Cucurbitaceae includes a variety of fruit crops with global or local economic importance. How their genomes evolve and the genetic basis of diversity remain largely unexplored. In this study, we sequence the genome of the wax gourd (<i>Benincasa hispida</i>), which bears giant fruit up to 80 cm in length and weighing over 20 kg. Comparative analyses of six cucurbit genomes reveal that the wax gourd genome represents the most ancestral karyotype, with the predicted ancestral genome having 15 proto-chromosomes. We also resequence 146 lines of diverse germplasm and build a variation map consisting of 16 million variations. Combining population genetics and linkage mapping, we identify a number of regions/genes potentially selected during domestication and improvement, some of which likely contribute to the large fruit size in wax gourds. Our analyses of these data help to understand genome evolution and function in cucurbits.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The family Cucurbitaceae (cucurbits) includes numerous economically important species, including those bearing edible and medicinal fruits, such as cucumber (<i>Cucumis sativus</i>), melon (<i>Cucumis melo</i>), watermelon (<i>Citrullus lanatus</i>), bottle gourd (<i>Lagenaria siceraria</i>), wax gourd (<i>Benincasa hispida</i>), pumpkin, and squash (<i>Cucurbita</i> spp.). Although these species are monophyletic, they display fascinating phenotypic variation in fruit characteristics. Comparing a few available genomes at that time for cucumber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275–1281 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d17209e672">1</a></sup>, melon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Garcia-Mas, J. et al. The genome of melon (Cucumis melo L.). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 11872–11877 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d17209e676">2</a></sup>, watermelon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Guo, S. et al. The draft genome of watermelon (Citrullus lanatus) and resequencing of 20 diverse accessions. Nat. Genet. 45, 51–58 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17209e680">3</a></sup>, and bottle gourd<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wu, S. et al. The bottle gourd genome provides insights into Cucurbitaceae evolution and facilitates mapping of a Papaya ring-spot virus resistance locus. Plant J. 92, 963–975 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17209e684">4</a></sup> previously proposed that the 12 chromosomes of melon may represent the ancestral karyotype of the cucurbit species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wu, S. et al. The bottle gourd genome provides insights into Cucurbitaceae evolution and facilitates mapping of a Papaya ring-spot virus resistance locus. Plant J. 92, 963–975 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17209e688">4</a></sup>. Some other species, such as wax gourd from the genus <i>Benincasa</i> and chayote (<i>Sechium edule</i>) from the tribe Sicyeae, also have 12 chromosomes. Whether these species have a similar karyotype, and whether they represent the ancestor of cucurbits remain to be determined. How these genomes evolve from their common ancestor remains to be resolved and requires analysis of the genomes of more cucurbit species.</p><p>Wax gourd, also known as ash gourd, white pumpkin, and white gourd, originated from the Indo-China region<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Robinson, R. W. &amp; Decker-Walters, D. S. Cucurbits 226 (Cab International, Wallingford, Oxon, New York, 1997)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d17209e702">5</a></sup> and is widely cultivated in India, Japan, China, and many other tropical areas, with increasing popularity in the Caribbean and the United States. Wild wax gourd has a small fruit (&lt;10 cm in length), whereas most wax gourd cultivars bear a giant fruit (up to 80 cm in length and weight of over 20 kg). Its fruit contains important nutrients, such as vitamins and flavonoids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Han, X. et al. New triterpenoids and other constituents from the fruits of Benincasa hispida (Thunb.) Cogn. J. Agric. Food Chem. 61, 12692–12699 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d17209e706">6</a></sup>, and metabolites that can be used in treating various disorders<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Grover, J. K., Adiga, G., Vats, V. &amp; Rathi, S. S. Extracts of Benincasa hispida prevent development of experimental ulcers. J. Ethnopharmacol. 78, 159–164 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d17209e710">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Gu, M. et al. Extract of wax gourd peel prevents high-fat diet-induced hyperlipidemia in C57BL/6 mice via the inhibition of the PPARgamma pathway. Evid. Based Complement Altern. Med. 2013, 1–11 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d17209e713">8</a></sup>.</p><p>The wax gourd is the only member of the genus <i>Benincasa</i> in the tribe <i>Benincaseae</i>, which includes cucumber, melon, watermelon, and bottle gourd. This situation, along with its large genome size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bennett, M. D., Smith, J. B. &amp; Heslop-Harrison, J. S. Nuclear DNA amounts in angiosperms. Proc. R. Soc. LondB 216, 179–199 (1982)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d17209e726">9</a></sup> and 12 chromosomes, as in melon, makes it an excellent system for exploring the evolution of cucurbit genomes. Currently, only a high-density genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiang, B. et al. High-density genetic map construction and gene mapping of pericarp color in wax gourd using specific-locus amplified fragment (SLAF) sequencing. BMC Genomics 16, 1035–1044 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17209e730">10</a></sup>, transcriptome sequences for several tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jiang, B. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the transcriptome, and development of SSR markers in wax gourd (Benicasa hispida). PLoS ONE 8, e71054 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17209e734">11</a></sup>, and a small number of genomic fragments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Bhave, Mrinal R., Gupta, Vidya S. &amp; Ranjekar, P. K. Arrangement and size distribution of repeat and single copy DNA sequences in four species of Cucurbitaceae. Plant Syst. Evol. 152, 133–151 (1986)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d17209e739">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Jiang, B. et al. Characterization and chromosomal distribution of Ty3-gypsy-like retrotransposons in wax gourd (Benincasa hispida). ScienceAsia 39, 466–471 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d17209e742">13</a></sup> have been developed for wax gourd. Hence, an assembled wax gourd genome is needed to facilitate a further investigation of cucurbit genome evolution and to explore the genetic basis of its diversity.</p><p>Here, we report a high-quality draft genome assembly of wax gourd cultivar B227, which is used to explore the genome evolution of cucurbit species, revealing their ancestral genome. Furthermore, by combining population analyses and genetic dissection, we identify genomic regions and genes, which may be involved in the determination of wax gourd fruit size. This wax gourd genome sequence and the genomic variation map offer valuable resources not only for facilitating wax gourd genetic research and improvement but also for studying evolution and speciation in the cucurbits.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and main features</h3><p>An inbred wax gourd line, B227, bearing large mature fruit (up to 80 cm in length) with dark green skin, was selected for genome sequencing, using Illumina and single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing technologies. A total of 55.4 Gb of high-quality, cleaned sequences were generated (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">1</a>), representing ~50-fold coverage of the estimated 1.03 Gb genome, based on k-mer analysis of the Illumina sequences (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">1</a>). Combing de novo assembly of Illumina and PacBio sequences yielded a draft genome of 913 Mb, with a scaffold N50 of 3.4 Mb length and longest scaffold of 14.5 Mb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">3</a>). Of the assembly, 859 Mb (94.1%), including 397 scaffolds, could be anchored to the 12 linkage groups, using the high-density genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiang, B. et al. High-density genetic map construction and gene mapping of pericarp color in wax gourd using specific-locus amplified fragment (SLAF) sequencing. BMC Genomics 16, 1035–1044 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17209e777">10</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">2a</a>). Marker order on the genetic map was significantly consistent with that on the genome assembly (<i>p</i> = 0.995–0.999, Pearson’s correlation coefficient; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">3</a>), indicating the high degree of accuracy of the assembly.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of wax gourd genome assembly and annotation</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The completeness of the wax gourd genome assembly was assessed using the BUSCO gene set<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Simao, F. A. et al. BUSCO: Assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d17209e857">14</a></sup> and available RNA-sequencing (RNA-seq) data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jiang, B. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the transcriptome, and development of SSR markers in wax gourd (Benicasa hispida). PLoS ONE 8, e71054 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17209e861">11</a></sup>. BUSCO analysis revealed that 93.2% of the core eukaryotic genes are present in the wax gourd genome, and 91.0% of them had complete coverage. In addition, 110 Gb of RNA-seq data obtained from five major tissue types (root, stem, young leaf, flower, and fruit) were mapped onto the wax gourd genome assembly. Overall, 94.6% of the RNA-seq reads could be mapped to the assembly. This extensive coverage of core eukaryotic genes, in conjunction with the high mapping rate of RNA-seq reads, indicated the high quality and overall completeness of the assembled genome.</p><p>A total of 689.5 Mb (75.5%) repetitive sequences and 27,467 protein-coding genes were predicted in the wax gourd genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">4</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">4</a>), Of these genes, 21,227 (77.28%) were annotated using known proteins (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">5</a>), and 19,972 (72.71%) could be supported by RNA-seq data from sampled tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jiang, B. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the transcriptome, and development of SSR markers in wax gourd (Benicasa hispida). PLoS ONE 8, e71054 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d17209e884">11</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Identification of the cucurbit ancestral genome</h3><p>To explore the genome evolution of wax gourd, genes from the seven cucurbits (wax gourd, cucumber, melon, watermelon, bottle gourd, pumpkin, and bitter gourd), three rosid species (soybean, <i>Arabidopsis</i>, and grape), one asterid (tomato), and one monocot (rice) were clustered into 28,232 gene families. Of these, 463 single-copy gene families were determined and used to reconstruct a maximum-likelihood phylogenetic tree (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig1">1a</a>). This revealed that wax gourd and the ancestor of watermelon and bottle gourd, which diverged about 16.3 million years ago (MYA), form a sister clade to <i>Cucumis</i> species, and the two clades diverged about 18.1 MYA, consistent with an earlier report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Schaefer, H., Heibl, C. &amp; Renner, S. S. Gourds afloat: a dated phylogeny reveals an Asian origin of the gourd family (Cucurbitaceae) and numerous oversea dispersal events. Proc. Biol. Sci. 276, 843–851 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d17209e905">15</a></sup>. The tribe <i>Benincaseae</i> was estimated to have diverged from the tribe <i>Cucurbiteae</i>, containing squash, and the tribe <i>Momordiceae</i>, containing bitter gourd, 26.4 and 36.1 MYA, respectively. In wax gourd, 32 gene families comprising 324 genes exhibited significant expansions (<i>p</i> &lt; 0.01) relative to their ancestor (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">1</a>). Some of these families were annotated as cytochrome b–c1 complex subunit, zinc-finger protein, and NBS-LRR resistance genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">6</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">5</a>). These genes might be a resource for investigating the specific features of wax gourd.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13185-3/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13185_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13185-3/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13185_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="704" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Phylogenetic relationship and comparative genomics analyses. <b>a</b> Phylogenetic tree of 12 plant species and evolution of gene families. Blue numerical value beside each node shows the estimated divergence time of each node (MYA, million years ago). Gene family: numbers of gene families in each species; gene in family: the numbers of genes that could be clustered into gene families; total gene: the total gene numbers for each species. <b>b</b> Distribution of synonymous substitution levels (Ks) of syntenic orthologous (solid curves) and paralogous genes (dashed curves) after evolutionary rate correction. Bhi: <i>Benincasa hispida</i>; Cma: <i>Cucurbit maxima</i>; Cme: <i>Cucumis melo</i>; Lsi: <i>Lagenaria siceraria</i>; Cla: <i>Citrullus lanatus</i>; Csa: <i>Cucumis sativus</i>; Vvi: <i>Vitis vinifera</i>. The source data underlying <b>a</b> is provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Whole-genome duplication (WGD) is thought to be a major driving force in evolution, as it provides additional genetic material that is then subject to divergence, sub-functionalization, and neofunctionalization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Van de Peer, Y., Maere, S. &amp; Meyer, A. The evolutionary significance of ancient genome duplications. Nat. Rev. Genet. 10, 725–732 (2009)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d17209e974">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lynch, M. &amp; Conery, J. S. The evolutionary fate and consequences of duplicate genes. Science 290, 1151–1155 (2000)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d17209e974_1">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Salman-Minkov, A., Sabath, N. &amp; Mayrose, I. Whole-genome duplication as a key factor in crop domestication. Nat. Plants 2, 16115 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d17209e977">18</a></sup>. To investigate WGD events in wax gourd, we identified syntenic blocks within its genome. The synonymous substitution rate (Ks) of collinear gene pairs indicated no recent WGD in wax gourd, and both the Eudicot-common hexaploidy (ECH)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d17209e981">19</a></sup> and the ancient cucurbit-common tetraploidization (CCT)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Wang, J. et al. An overlooked paleotetraploidization in Cucurbitaceae. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 16–26 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d17209e985">20</a></sup> events were observed (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig1">1b</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">6a, b</a>). This situation is similar to other species of the tribe <i>Benincaseae</i>, including cucumber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275–1281 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d17209e999">1</a></sup>, melon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Garcia-Mas, J. et al. The genome of melon (Cucumis melo L.). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 11872–11877 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1003">2</a></sup>, watermelon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Guo, S. et al. The draft genome of watermelon (Citrullus lanatus) and resequencing of 20 diverse accessions. Nat. Genet. 45, 51–58 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1007">3</a></sup>, and bottle gourd<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wu, S. et al. The bottle gourd genome provides insights into Cucurbitaceae evolution and facilitates mapping of a Papaya ring-spot virus resistance locus. Plant J. 92, 963–975 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1011">4</a></sup>, but distinct from pumpkin and squash genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Sun, H. et al. Karyotype stability and unbiased fractionation in the paleo-allotetraploid Cucurbita genomes. Mol. Plant 10, 1293–1306 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1015">21</a></sup> in the tribe <i>Cucurbiteae</i> with a recent WGD (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig1">1b</a>). In addition, the Ks distribution also suggests divergent evolutionary rates for these cucurbit species (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">6a</a>). After correction using grape, as a reference to eliminate the influence of the ECH event, wax gourd and melon appear to have the slowest evolutionary rates (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">6b</a>). Thus, the wax gourd genome has no recent WGD and has the slowest evolutionary rate among the cucurbits.</p><p>To infer the chromosome evolution of wax gourd (<i>n</i> = 12) and other cucurbits, including cucumber (<i>n</i> = 7)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275–1281 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1041">1</a></sup>, melon (<i>n</i> = 12)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Garcia-Mas, J. et al. The genome of melon (Cucumis melo L.). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 11872–11877 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1048">2</a></sup>, watermelon (<i>n</i> = 11)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Guo, S. et al. The draft genome of watermelon (Citrullus lanatus) and resequencing of 20 diverse accessions. Nat. Genet. 45, 51–58 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1056">3</a></sup>, bottle gourd (<i>n</i> = 11)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wu, S. et al. The bottle gourd genome provides insights into Cucurbitaceae evolution and facilitates mapping of a Papaya ring-spot virus resistance locus. Plant J. 92, 963–975 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1063">4</a></sup>, and pumpkin (<i>n</i> = 20)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Sun, H. et al. Karyotype stability and unbiased fractionation in the paleo-allotetraploid Cucurbita genomes. Mol. Plant 10, 1293–1306 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1070">21</a></sup>, we identified syntenic blocks across their genomes, using wax gourd as the reference (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig2">2a</a>). Analysis of the syntenic relationships shows that the integrity of six wax gourd chromosomes (chromosomes 2, 3, 5, 7, 8, and 10) was essentially preserved in pumpkin, from tribe <i>Cucurbiteae</i>, as well as in other species from tribe <i>Benincaseae</i>. The 12 melon chromosomes were previously proposed as the most ancestral karyotype from the then available cucurbit genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wu, S. et al. The bottle gourd genome provides insights into Cucurbitaceae evolution and facilitates mapping of a Papaya ring-spot virus resistance locus. Plant J. 92, 963–975 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1084">4</a></sup>; however, only five melon chromosomes (chromosomes 2, 8, 9, 10, and 12) were well preserved in the pumpkin genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">7</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13185-3/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13185_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13185-3/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13185_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="961" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Genome evolution of the six sequenced cucurbit species. <b>a</b> Conserved genome karyotype of wax gourd and identification of ancestral chromosomes of cucurbit species. Different colors along the 12 wax gourd chromosomes indicate the origin of the 15 ancestral chromosomes. Bhi: <i>Benincasa hispida</i>; Cma: <i>Cucurbit maxima</i>; Cme: <i>Cucumis melo</i>; Lsi: <i>Lagenaria siceraria</i>; Cla: <i>Citrullus lanatus</i>; Csa: <i>Cucumis sativus</i>. <b>b</b> Evolutionary scenario of the Cucurbitaceae genomes from the ancestral Cucurbitaceae karyotype. sWGD: specific whole-genome duplication; CCT: cucurbit-common tetraploidization</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Next, we analyzed the syntenic relationships of the wax gourd and melon genomes vs. the bottle gourd genome, which has the most preserved ancestral karyotype among cucurbits after melon. Four wax gourd chromosomes (chromosomes 2, 3, 10, and 12) show nearly one-to-one syntenic relationships with bottle gourd chromosomes, whereas only two (chromosomes 2 and 8) show such a relationship in melon (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">8</a>). Given the lowest evolutionary rate of wax gourd among the sequenced cucurbits, these data support the hypothesis that wax gourd represents the most ancestral genome among these species.</p><p>The ancestral chromosomes were inferred on the basis of syntenic relationships among cucurbit genomes, using the wax gourd as reference (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig2">2a</a>). The six wax gourd chromosomes (Bhi 2, 3, 5, 7, 8, and 10), showing a one-to-one relationship with the pumpkin genome, were considered as proto-chromosomes before the cucurbit-common WGD. Despite the integrity of wax gourd chromosome 4 (Bhi 4) is not being preserved in pumpkin, it has a one-to-one syntenic relationship with melon chromosome 3, indicating that it is also a proto-chromosome. The seven wax gourd chromosomes (Bhi 2, 3, 4, 5, 7, 8, and 10) were ordinally named proto-chromosomes 1–7. In addition, large patches of chromosome segments shared by extant genomes can be used to infer other proto-chromosomes. For example, wax gourd chromosome Bhi 9 could be found to occur in partite manner in other genomes, and each part is independent of the other one, and at the mean time independent of other chromosomes; this leads to the definition of proto-chromosomes 8 and 9. Similarly, we inferred proto-chromosomes 11, 12, 14, and 15. Some large patches have linked co-existence in extant genomes, showing that they could have originated from the same proto-chromosome. For example, one patch in Bhi 1 and another in Bhi 11 co-occurs in four genomes, especially two times in Cma, showing that they should be from the same proto-chromosome; this leads to the inference of proto-chromosome 13. Similarly, we inferred proto-chromosome 10. These data suggest that the ancestral genome of these sequenced cucurbit genomes has 15 proto-chromosomes.</p><p>The evolution of chromosomes in other sequenced cucurbits was investigated assuming the 15 ancestral chromosomes to have served as their origin (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig2">2b</a>). After wax gourd, the melon genome best preserved the ancestral karyotype of cucurbits, as previously reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wu, S. et al. The bottle gourd genome provides insights into Cucurbitaceae evolution and facilitates mapping of a Papaya ring-spot virus resistance locus. Plant J. 92, 963–975 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1143">4</a></sup>, with seven melon chromosomes (chromosomes 2, 3, 6, 8, 9, 10, and 12) derived directly from the ancestral ones. Despite the recent WGD in the pumpkin genome, 6 (chromosomes 3, 7, 10, 13, 19, and 20) of the 20 chromosomes remained in the ancestral state. The bottle gourd genome retained three ancestral chromosomes (chromosomes 1, 6, and 10), whereas all chromosomes of cucumber and watermelon were formed through a number of fusions and fissions. This information will be fundamental for comparative genomics in cucurbits.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Repeat expansion leads to large genome size in cucurbits</h3><p>The number of protein-coding genes and highly conserved syntenic blocks in the wax gourd genome are comparable with those in the genomes of other sequenced species in the tribe <i>Benincaseae</i>, including cucumber, melon, and watermelon (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">9</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">7</a>). However, the assembled genome size of wax gourd (913.0 Mb) is at least twofold larger than that of the other three species (200.0–400.0 Mb). The absence of a recent WGD event in wax gourd suggests that this large genome size did not result from a specific WGD event (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig1">1b</a>).</p><p>Comparing the content of various repeats in the four species indicates that the length of DNA transposons and long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons, including <i>Copia</i>, <i>Gypsy</i>, and other elements, in wax gourd is much greater than that in the other three species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig3">3a</a>). For example, the length of <i>Copia</i> elements in wax gourd is ~20-fold longer than that in cucumber and nine-fold longer than that in melon and watermelon (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">8</a>). Therefore, the substantial accumulation of transposable elements (TEs) and especially LTR retrotransposons contributes greatly to the large genome size of wax gourd.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13185-3/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13185_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13185-3/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13185_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="924" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Expansion of repeats in wax gourd genome. <b>a</b> Transposable elements content in cucumber, melon, watermelon, and wax gourd genomes. <b>b</b> Distribution of insertion times for LTR retrotransposons in cucumber, melon, watermelon, and wax gourd. Phylogenetic relationships of <i>Copia</i> (<b>c</b>) and <i>Gypsy</i> (<b>d</b>) retrotransposons across cucumber, melon, watermelon, and wax gourd. Source data are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To trace the history of the greatly expanded repetitive sequences in wax gourd, we estimated insertion times and analyzed the phylogenetic relationships of LTR retrotransposons, the most abundant repeats, using the 7136 full-length LTRs predicted in the wax gourd genome. LTRs accumulated gradually in the wax gourd genome before the divergence (~16.3 MYA) of wax gourd and watermelon, and peaked at around 9 MYA after speciation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig3">3b</a>). LTRs accumulated earlier and faster in the wax gourd genome than in the three other <i>Benincaseae</i> species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig3">3b</a>).</p><p>The recent substantial proliferation of LTRs in cucumber is not observed in wax gourd. In addition, we inferred phylogenies for the reverse transcriptase (RT) domain of both <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i> elements (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig3">3c, d</a>). A number of diverse and ancient LTR subfamilies are present in all four species, along with numerous species-specific LTRs, especially in the wax gourd genome. Most LTRs were greatly expanded in wax gourd after speciation, and this ancient species-specific process led to the large extant genome of wax gourd.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genomic variations and population structure of wax gourd</h3><p>To explore genetic variations in the wax gourd germplasm, 146 wax gourd accessions, including 13 wild accessions, 16 landraces, and 117 cultivated accessions, were selected and re-sequenced using Illumina sequencing technology (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">10</a>). We generated 2.9 Tb of high-quality, cleaned sequences with an average ~15.68-fold and 95.38% coverage rate of the wax gourd genome (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM5">2</a>). Mapping the reads onto the wax gourd genome identified a final set of 16,183,153 high-quality single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM5">3</a>) and 2,190,214 small insertions and deletions (InDels). Among the SNPs, 170,365 are missense SNPs, 2047 are nonsense and 1258 are located at splice site acceptors or donors. This variation data set represents a new resource for wax gourd biology and genetic breeding.</p><p>To infer the population structure of wax gourd germplasm, phylogenetic, Bayesian clustering and principal component (PCA) analyses were performed using fourfold-degenerate sites (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig4">4</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">12</a>). All results support clustering of wax gourd accessions into four groups. The 13 wild accessions form a wild group (W) clade with some admixtures. Of the 16 landraces, 14 group together as a landrace group (L), with some admixtures from wild and cultivated accessions. The remaining 117 cultivated and two landrace accessions belong to the cultivated group and could be divided into two distinct sub-groups, one with fruit wax (sub-group C1) and the other without (C2).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13185-3/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13185_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13185-3/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13185_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="880" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Population structure of 146 wax gourd accessions. <b>a</b> Fruit morphology of the four groups. Bar is 5 cm. <b>b</b> Neighbor-joining phylogenetic tree constructed using SNPs at fourfold-degenerate sites. Wild group (W) contains wide accessions from India, Japan, and China (orange); accessions of Landrace group (L) are mainly form Xishuangbanna region of China (green). Two cultivated groups contain accessions bearing large fruit, with group C1 (blue) being covered by wax, but not in group C2 (magenta). <b>c</b> Model-based clustering analysis with different cluster numbers (<i>k</i> = 2, 3, and 4). The <i>y</i>-axis quantifies cluster membership, and the <i>x</i>-axis lists the different accessions. The orders and positions of these accessions on the <i>x</i>-axis are consistent with those in the neighbor-joining tree. The source data underlying <b>b</b> and <b>c</b> are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The phylogenetic tree suggests that cultivated wax gourds were most likely formed through a two-step evolutionary process, including domestication from wild to landrace accessions and improvement from landrace to cultivated accessions (sub-groups C1 and C2). Sub-group C2 without fruit wax should have been bred from sub-group C1 with fruit wax. As in other cucurbits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Qi, J. et al. A genomic variation map provides insights into the genetic basis of cucumber domestication and diversity. Nat. Genet. 45, 1510–1515 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1305">22</a></sup>, the genome-wide nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) of the wild group (5.9 × 10<sup>−3</sup>) is far higher than that of the landrace (1.1 × 10<sup>−3</sup>) and cultivated groups (0.4 × 10<sup>−3</sup>), indicating a highly diverse gene pool in the wild group, which could be a valuable genetic resource for wax gourd improvement.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Candidate regions/genes conferring fruit size under selection</h3><p>For many crops, an essential change during the process of domestication and improvement was the increase of fruit and/or seed size. The fruit mass of wax gourd was increased from ~0.5 kg in the wild accessions to ~2.0 kg in the landrace accessions, during domestication, and subsequently from ~2.0 kg in the landrace accessions to ~10 kg in the cultivated accessions during improvement. Histological and morphological analyses of the fruit between landrace accession B214 with fruit of ~2.0 kg and cultivated accession B227 with fruit of ~20 kg indicate that increased fruit size is determined by both number and volume of cells (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">13</a>).</p><p>To identify potential selective signals related to fruit size and other important agronomic traits, during wax gourd domestication (wild vs. landrace) and improvement (landrace vs. cultivated), we scanned genomic regions with a drastic reduction of nucleotide diversity (top 10%). The sweep regions were further filtered using top 50% XP-CLR scores<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Chen, H., Patterson, N. &amp; Reich, D. Population differentiation as a test for selective sweeps. Genome Res. 20, 393–402 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1333">23</a></sup>. Finally, we detected 234 domestication sweeps ranging from 200 to 2580 kb in length (456 kb on average) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig5">5a</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM5">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM5">5</a>) and 168 improvement sweeps (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig5">5b</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM5">6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM5">7</a>) ranging from 200 to 2440 kb in length (475 kb on average). The domestication and improvement sweeps occupy 106.7 Mb (11.6% of the assembly genome), including 3939 genes, and 80.3 Mb (9.1%), including 2251 genes, respectively.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13185-3/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13185_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13185-3/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13185_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="644" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Domestication/improvement selective sweeps and identification of fruit-size candidate genes in wax gourd. <b>a</b>, <b>b</b> Genome-wide distribution of selective sweeps during domestication and improvement. Red vertical boxes illustrate selective sweeps and light green bars illustrate QTLs related fruit size. <b>c</b> Differential expression of candidate genes related to fruit size between large (B227) and small (B214) fruited accessions, at three developmental stages. <b>d</b> Boxplots show the epidermal pavement cell size of wax gourd cotyledon with SAUR overexpression and empty vector (OE: overexpression), <i>p</i> value was calculated using one-sided Student’s <i>t</i> test. <b>e</b> A major fruit length QTL on chromosome 10. <b>f</b> Domestication sweeps within the genetically mapped QTL interval. Horizontal blue lines indicate sweep regions. The source data underlying  <b>a</b>, <b>b</b>, and <b>d</b> are provided as a Source Data file</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Of note, on chromosome 10, a domestication sweep region from 47.84 to 48.34 Mb contains 21 genes. Among these genes, <i>Bhi10G001538</i> encoding a small auxin-up-regulated RNA (SAUR) is significantly up-regulated in the large-fruited accession (B227), at three developmental stages, compared to that in the small-fruited one, and is highly expressed specifically in the fruit (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig5">5c</a>). In cucumber, its ortholog <i>Csa2G258100</i> is also significantly up-regulated in long-fruited line<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Jiang, L. et al. Transcriptomic analysis reveals the roles of microtubule-related genes and transcription factors in fruit length regulation in cucumber (Cucumis sativus L.). Sci. Rep. 5, 8031 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1410">24</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">14</a>), and was mapped in the quantitative trait locus (QTL) interval <i>fd2.1</i> for fruit diameter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Pan, Y. et al. QTL mapping of domestication and diversifying selection related traits in round-fruited semi-wild Xishuangbanna cucumber (Cucumis sativus L. var. xishuangbannanesis). Theor. Appl. Genet. 130, 1531–1548 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1421">25</a></sup>. Moreover, SAUR was reported to be involved in regulating plant growth and development by promoting cell expansion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ren, H. &amp; Gray, W. M. SAUR proteins as effectors of hormonal and environmental signals in plant growth. Mol. Plant 8, 1153–1164 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1425">26</a></sup>. To confirm the function of <i>Bhi10G001538</i> gene in cell expansion in wax gourd, we transiently expressed <i>35S-MYC</i>-<i>Bhi10G001538</i>, and a vector control, in cotyledons by agroinfiltration. These assays indicated that the cell size of cotyledonary epidermal pavement cells, at 5 days post infiltration (dpi), was significantly larger than that in cotyledons expressing a vector control (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig5">5d</a>). Therefore, <i>Bhi10G001538</i> may be an important candidate domestication gene conferring large fruit size in wax gourd.</p><p>To further explore for genes potentially related to fruit size, during domestication and improvement, we performed QTL mapping of fruit-size-associated traits, using a segregating population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Liu, W. et al. Genetic analysis and QTL mapping of fruit-related traits in wax gourd (Benincasa hispida). Euphytica 214, 136–143 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1448">27</a></sup> derived from a cross between landrace accession B214, which should be an admixture of wild, landrace, and cultivated wax gourds (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig4">4c</a>), and a cultivated accession, B227. We mapped nine QTLs, including two for fruit mass (<i>fw3.1</i> and <i>fw6.1</i>), two for fruit length (<i>fl4.1</i> and <i>fl10.1</i>), two for fruit diameter (<i>fd3.1</i> and <i>fd11.1</i>), and three for thickness of fruit flesh (<i>ft3.1</i>, <i>ft5.1</i>, and <i>ft9.1</i>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig5">5</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM5">8</a>). Among these nine QTLs, six (<i>fw3.1</i>, <i>fd3.1</i>, <i>ft3.1</i>, <i>ft5.1, fl10.1</i>, and <i>fd11.1</i>) contain both domestication and improvement sweeps, with the selection occurring at different positions; these regions may harbor multiple genes related to fruit size. For instance, within the physical interval of the <i>fl10.1</i> QTL for fruit length, one domestication sweep from 5.20 to 6.56 Mb contains 55 genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig5">5e, f</a>). Among these genes, <i>Bhi10G000196</i> is the homolog of <i>SlFIN</i> (<i>Solyc11g064850</i>) in tomato, the mutation of which can cause enlarged tomato fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Xu, C. et al. A cascade of arabinosyltransferases controls shoot meristem size in tomato. Nat. Genet. 47, 784–795 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1522">28</a></sup>. Moreover, <i>Bhi10G000196</i> is significantly down-regulated in the large-fruited accession, at three developmental stages, compared to that in the small-fruited accession (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#Fig5">5c</a>) and highly expressed in the fruit. These data support a role for this gene in the increase of fruit size during wax gourd domestication.</p><p>Genome-wide association studies (GWASs) were also performed for traits related to fruit size, including fruit weight, length, diameter, and thickness of fruit flesh, using the 146 accessions sequenced. Here, we identified 30 significant association signals at a threshold of −log<sub>10</sub>(<i>p</i>) = 6 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">15</a> and Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM5">9</a>). Among them, 11 overlapped with domestication/improvement sweeps containing 36 genes. Thus, these 11 genes also provide candidate domestication genes responsible for large fruit size.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>In this study, we present a high-quality draft genome sequence for wax gourd, which has a larger genome size than other cucurbit species, such as cucumber, melon, and watermelon. Comparative analysis of the available whole genomes of cucurbit species provides evidence in support of the model in which the wax gourd genome is the most ancestral karyotype of the cucurbit species investigated. Wax gourd belongs to a monotypic genus in which no new speciation occurred. In mammals, taxa with low rates of speciation are associated with the so-called cold genomes, which have lower TE activity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Ricci, M. et al. Transposable elements activity is positively related to rate of speciation in mammals. J. Mol. Evol. 86, 303–310 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1556">29</a></sup>. Considering that the large proportion of repetitive sequences in wax gourd are ancient TEs with low activity and TE activity is associated with speciation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Presgraves, D. C. The molecular evolutionary basis of species formation. Nat. Rev. Genet. 11, 175–180 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1560">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Serrato-Capuchina, A. &amp; Matute, D. R. The role of transposable elements in speciation. Genes (Basel) 9, 1–29 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1563">31</a></sup>, we speculate that the large number of ancient repeats in wax gourd, which has the slowest evolutionary rate among cucurbits, might be related to the ancestral karyotype and the nature of the monotypic genus.</p><p>All analyzed species here are from the tribe <i>Benincaseae</i> and <i>Cucurbiteae</i>. There are a total of 15 tribes in the Cucurbitaceae family. With the availability of more genome sequences of cucurbit species from other tribes, the accurate ancestral genome and the evolutionary scenario of cucurbit genomes will be investigated comprehensively. It seems that the species with 12 chromosomes such as wax gourd and melon were preferred to preserve the ancestral chromosomes. Chayote (<i>Sechium edule</i>) is a popular fruit plant from the tribe <i>Sicyeae</i>, and it also has 12 chromosomes. Thus, the genome of chayote may provide insights into the genome evolution of cucurbits.</p><p>Most wax gourd cultivars bear a giant fruit, but its wild form has a small fruit. How wax gourd domesticated from wild to cultivated one remains largely unknown. By resequencing 146 accessions, we generated a genomic variation map for wax gourd and revealed its population structure and the genetic basis of diversity. A number of putative genome regions under domestication or improvement were identified. Several candidate genes were proposed to be involved in the process from wild small fruit to cultivated large one. Interestingly, homologs in other species of <i>Bhi10G001538</i> and <i>Bhi10G000196</i> are also probably responsible for fruit size.</p><p>In summary, the draft genome sequence and genomic variation map of wax gourd provide insights into the genome evolution of cucurbit species and the genomic basis of wax gourd’s diversity. Moreover, these data and information are valuable resources for wax gourd research and breeding, and for comparative genomic analysis of cucurbit species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Sequencing and assembly of the wax gourd genome</h3><p>For genome sequencing, genomic DNA was extracted from leaf buds of wax gourd inbred line B227 using a modified cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Murray, M. G. &amp; Thompson, W. F. Rapid isolation of high molecular weight plant DNA. Nucleic Acids Res. 8, 4321–4325 (1980)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1606">32</a></sup> protocol. Three paired-end (with insert sizes of 180 and 500 bp) and five mate-pair sequencing DNA libraries (with insert sizes of 2 and 8 kb) were constructed following the Illumina standard protocol (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">1</a>). A total of 29.3 Gb paired-end and 12.1 Gb mate-pair sequences was generated on sequencing platform Illumina Hiseq 2000. All low-quality reads were filtered. In addition, ~15 G subreads were generated using SMRT sequencing technology on the PacBio RSII platform. The average length of the subreads was 6.1 kb.</p><p>The Illumina paired-end reads were assembled into contigs using the ALLPATHS-LG-44837 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Butler, J. et al. ALLPATHS: de novo assembly of whole-genome shotgun microreads. Genome Res. 18, 810–820 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1616">33</a></sup>. These contigs were then connected into scaffolds based on the mate-pair reads. The gaps in the preliminary assembly were filled by integrating PacBio subreads with PBjelly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS ONE 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1620">34</a></sup>. Furthermore, we used the ALLMAPS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps. Genome Biol. 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1624">35</a></sup> software to anchor those scaffolds onto 12 pseudo-chromosomes, based on a published high-density genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jiang, B. et al. High-density genetic map construction and gene mapping of pericarp color in wax gourd using specific-locus amplified fragment (SLAF) sequencing. BMC Genomics 16, 1035–1044 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1628">10</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome annotation</h3><p>RepeatModeler (v1.0.4) was used for de novo prediction of TEs in the wax gourd genome. These de novo predicted repeats, together with the TIGR plant repeats database (<a href="http://plantrepeats.plantbiology.msu.edu">http://plantrepeats.plantbiology.msu.edu</a>), were used to mask the repeats in the wax gourd genome. These masked repeats were then classified into different types based on the annotation of RepeatMasker.</p><p>Evidence from transcript mapping, ab initio gene prediction, and homologous gene alignment was combined to predict protein-coding genes in the repeat-masked wax gourd genome. RNA-seq data from five tissues were aligned against the wax gourd genome, using HISAT2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Pertea, M. et al. Transcript-level expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with HISAT, StringTie and Ballgown. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1650–1667 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1647">36</a></sup>, and were assembled using stringtie (1.2.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Pertea, M. et al. Transcript-level expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with HISAT, StringTie and Ballgown. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1650–1667 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1651">36</a></sup>. The assembled transcripts were further processed by PASA (v2.0.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Brian, J. H. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1655">37</a></sup>, and then were used for ab initio prediction as well as the evidence of transcript mapping. SNAP (2006-07-28)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics. 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1659">38</a></sup>, GlimmerHMM (v2.0.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878–2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1663">39</a></sup>, and AUGUSTUS (v3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Stanke, M., Diekhans, M., Baertsch, R. &amp; Haussler, D. Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics 24, 637–644 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1668">40</a></sup> were used for ab initio gene prediction. For homologous gene alignment, non-redundant plant protein sequences, downloaded from Uniprot (<a href="http://www.uniprot.org">http://www.uniprot.org</a>), were aligned to the genome using Wise (2.4.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1676">41</a></sup>. Finally, gene structures were predicted on the basis of a weighted consensus of all the evidence using EVM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1680">42</a></sup>.</p><p>All predicted proteins were aligned to GenBank NR, the <i>Arabidopsis</i> protein, UniProt (Swiss-Prot), and InterPro databases using BLAST or interproscan (5.16–55.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Hunter, S. et al. InterPro: the integrative protein signature database. Nucleic Acids Res 37, D211–D215 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1690">43</a></sup> for functional annotation of protein-coding genes. Based on alignment results, the GO and Uniprot annotations were assigned for each protein.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Gene families and phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>We used the OrthoMCL package (version 2.0.9)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1702">44</a></sup> to identify gene families/clusters between the wax gourd and 11 other plant species, including six other cucurbits (cucumber, melon, watermelon, bottle gourd, pumpkin, bitter gourd), three rosid species (soybean, <i>Arabidopsis</i>, grape), one asteroid (tomato), and one monocot (rice). We investigated the dynamic evolution of gene families using Cafe ́software (version 3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Han, M. V., Thomas, G. W., Lugo-Martinez, J. &amp; Hahn, M. W. Estimating gene gain and loss rates in the presence of error in genome assembly and annotation using CAFE 3. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1987–1997 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1709">45</a></sup> with a probabilistic graphical model. Phylogenetic relationship among these 12 plant species was resolved using the RAxML package (version 8.1.13)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1713">46</a></sup>, based on the 463 high-quality single-copy orthologous genes. Divergence times were estimated by the program MCMCtree in PAML (version 3.15) (<a href="http://abacus.gene.ucl.ac.uk/software/paml.html">http://abacus.gene.ucl.ac.uk/software/paml.html</a>), based on known divergence time between cucumber and melon (about 10 MYA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Sebastian, P., Schaefer, H., Telford, I. R. &amp; Renner, S. S. Cucumber (Cucumis sativus) and melon (C. melo) have numerous wild relatives in Asia and Australia, and the sister species of melon is from Australia. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci.
