USA 107, 14269–14273 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1722">47</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Gene collinearity and Ks analysis</h3><p>Protein sequences within a genome or between different genomes were aligned by BLASTP. Matched genes with <i>e</i> value &lt;1e − 5 were considered as potential homologous genes. Next, syntenic blocks within a genome or between different genomes were determined based on the detected homologous gene pairs using ColinearScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Wang, X. et al. Statistical inference of chromosomal homology based on gene colinearity and applications to Arabidopsis and rice. BMC Bioinformatics. 7, 447 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1737">48</a></sup>. WGD events were inferred from the syntenic relationships within a genome.</p><p>Synonymous nucleotide substitutions on synonymous sites (Ks) were estimated using the Nei–Gojobori approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Nei, M. &amp; Gojobori, T. Simple methods for estimating the numbers of synonymous and nonsynonymous nucleotide substitutions. Mol. Biol. Evol. 3, 418–426 (1986)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1744">49</a></sup> implemented in the Bioperl Statistical module. We used normal distribution to represent the complex Ks distribution, and the principle one was used to represent the corresponding evolutionary event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Wang, J. et al. An overlooked paleotetraploidization in Cucurbitaceae. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 16–26 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1748">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Wang, J. et al. Hierarchically aligning 10 legume genomes establishes a family-level genomics platform. Plant Physiol. 174, 284–300 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1751">50</a></sup>. To infer the evolutionary events and rates properly, we corrected the Ks on the basis of ECH and CCT WGD events. First, the inferred Ks peak from ECH-produced duplicated genes was aligned to have the same value with that of grape, which has been evolved the slowest. Then, all related cucurbits have the same distribution peak at the CCT event with that of wax gourd, which has the slowest evolutionary rate among these cucurbits.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Evolutionary scenario of cucurbit genomes</h3><p>By comparing different cucurbit genomes, phylogenetically, we adopted a bottom-up approach to reconstruct the ancestral cell karyotypes of cucurbit plants. First, by inferring putative homologous genes and collinear genes, we drew homologous gene dot plots within a genome and between genomes. Ks values were estimated to infer collinear genes produced by different events, and the information was integrated into the dot plots. Second, since pumpkin is the outgroup of other studied cucurbits, we checked the dot plots to assess whether its chromosomes or main structures of its chromosomes were shared by other cucurbit plants. Third, the fusion and fission events during genome evolution of cucurbit species from their ancestral chromosomes were determined.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Analysis of full-length LTR retrotransposons</h3><p>We used LTR_Finder (v1.0.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1772">51</a></sup> to de novo detect full-length LTR retrotransposons in four species of Cucurbitaceae (cucumber, melon, watermelon, and wax gourd) genomes, with the following command line ltr_finder genome.fa -s tRNAdb/Athal-tRNAs.fa -a ps_scan &gt; result.txt. Next, we obtained candidate full-length LTRs by filtering those that overlapped. These full-length LTR retrotransposons were then translated into amino acids, in six frames, with the best being selected. Their functional domains were predicted using the software HMMER, based on the Pfam database. Paralogs of the RT domain, specific for the <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i> super-families, were then detected based on the functional domains and their orders.</p><p>The RT protein sequences were aligned using MUSCLE (v3.8.31)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1785">52</a></sup> and neighbor-joining (NJ) trees were built using MEGA (default parameters) for the <i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i> super-families. The two ends of these LTR retrotransposons were aligned with MUSCLE, and the nucleotide distance (<i>D</i>) was estimated using the Kimura two-parameter (K2p) (transition–transversion ratio) criterion, as implemented in the distmat program in the EMBOSS package (v6.6.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Rice, P., Longden, I. &amp; Bleasby, A. EMBOSS: the European Molecular Biology Open Software Suite. Trends Genet 16, 276–277 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1798">53</a></sup>. Then rate of nucleotide substitution (<i>μ</i>) were inferred as following strategy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579–584 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1806">54</a></sup>: first, detecting LTR-NRR (not repeat-related flanking sequence) orthologous insert sequences between cucumber and melon; second, estimating nucleotide distance (<i>D</i>) of orthologous LTRs between cucumber and melon; finally, substitution rates (<i>μ</i>) were inferred using the formula: <i>μ</i> = <i>D</i>/2<i>T</i> based on the known divergence time between cucumber and melon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Sebastian, P., Schaefer, H., Telford, I. R. &amp; Renner, S. S. Cucumber (Cucumis sativus) and melon (C. melo) have numerous wild relatives in Asia and Australia, and the sister species of melon is from Australia. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 14269–14273 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1826">47</a></sup>. The insertion time (<i>T</i>) of an LTR retrotransposon was calculated using Eq. 1:</p><div id="Equ1" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${T} = {D}/2{\upmu},$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (1)
                </div></div><p>where <i>μ</i> is 4.5e<sup>−9</sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome variation map of wax gourd</h3><p>A total of 146 wax gourd accessions were included in this study, all being collected by Guangdong Academy of Agricultural Sciences. Genomic DNA was extracted from leaf buds using the CTAB method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Murray, M. G. &amp; Thompson, W. F. Rapid isolation of high molecular weight plant DNA. Nucleic Acids Res. 8, 4321–4325 (1980)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1863">32</a></sup>. Paired-end Illumina genomic libraries with insert sizes of 300–500 bp were prepared and sequenced on an Illumina Hiseq4000 platform (Illumina Inc., USA) with the read length of 150 bp.</p><p>The paired-end sequence reads from each accession were mapped onto the wax gourd reference genome, using BWA (version: 0.7.17-r1188)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1870">55</a></sup> with the default parameters. SAMtools (version: 1.6-3-g200708f)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1874">56</a></sup> was used to convert mapping results into the BAM format, to sort mapping results according to mapping coordinates and to remove PCR duplicated reads. The resultant files were then used in the following procedures for variant detection.</p><p>Variant calling was performed using the SNP detection procedure of the Genome Analysis Toolkit (version: v3.2-2-gec30cee)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1881">57</a></sup>. These SNPs were further filtered using the following criteria: (i) one position with more than two alleles was considered to be a polymorphic site in the population; (ii) total sequencing depth had to be &gt;150 and &lt;6570, the sequences with the depth &gt;6570 were considered as repeat sequences; (iii) nearest SNPs had to be &gt;1 bp away; and (iv) we filtered out sites at which &lt;85% of the lines appeared to be homozygous and sites with a proportion of heterozygous genotypes greater than three times that of the homozygous genotypes with the minor allele. In addition, small InDels (≤5 bp in length) were also identified with the same criteria.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Phylogenetic and population analysis</h3><p>To build a NJ tree, we screened a subset of 6585 SNPs at fourfold-degenerate sites (minor allele frequency (MAF) &gt;5% and missing data &lt;10%) from the identified SNPs of the wax gourd accessions. These SNPs should be under lower selective pressure, thus they are considered to be more reliable in reflecting population structure and demography. We constructed a phylogenetic tree using MEGA (version 6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA6: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis version 6.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2725–2729 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1893">58</a></sup> with 1000 bootstrap replicates.</p><p>Using the same SNP data set, we also investigated the population structure using STRUCTURE (version 2.3.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Falush, D., Stephens, M. &amp; Pritchard, J. K. Inference of population structure using multilocus genotype data: linked loci and correlated allele frequencies. Genetics 164, 1567–1587 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1900">59</a></sup>, on the basis of allele frequencies. To determine the most likely group number, STRUCTURE was run 20 times on 1000 randomly selected SNPs at fourfold-degenerate sites for each <i>K</i> value from 2 to 20. After determining Δ<i>K</i>, we used 6585 SNPs at fourfold-degenerate sites to determine the group membership of each accession by 10,000 iterations, with <i>K</i> values from 2 to 4. In addition, we performed PCA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Patterson, N., Price, A. L. &amp; Reich, D. Population structure and eigenanalysis. PLoS Genet 2, e190 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1913">60</a></sup> using the same data set. Two-dimensional coordinates were plotted for the 146 wax gourd accessions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Identification of domestication and improvement sweeps</h3><p>Nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) and Tajima’s <i>D</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Tajima, F. Evolutionary relationship of DNA sequences in finite populations. Genetics 105, 437–460 (1983)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1930">61</a></sup> in wild (W), landrace (L), and cultivated (W) wax gourd groups were calculated on the basis of the genotypes of each accession using BioPerl. A sliding window approach was used to calculate <i>π</i> in the wax gourd genome with a window size of 200 kb and a step size of 20 kb. By scanning the ratios of genetic diversity between W and L (<i>π</i><sub>W</sub>/<i>π</i><sub>L</sub> domestication step), as well as between L and C (<i>π</i><sub>L</sub>/<i>π</i><sub>C</sub> improvement step), we selected windows with the top 10% of ratios (97.3 and 20.9 for domestication and improvement, respectively) as candidate domestication/improvement regions. To improve the predictive accuracy, only candidate selective sweeps having the top 50% of XP-CLR scores were kept. Finally, windows that were ≤200 kb apart were merged into a single region under selection.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Measurement and statistics of cell size</h3><p>To measure cell size in the wax gourd fruit of B227 and B214, 1-cm-thick sliced samples were cut from the outer, middle, and inner pericarp at different developmental timepoints, at 0, 5, 10, 15, 20, and 25 days after pollination (DAP). These sections were fixed in a solution of ethanol (70%), acetic acid, and formaldehyde (90:5:5 by volume) and then embedded into paraffin. Subsequently, 8-μm-thick-microtome sections were prepared (from cross and longitudinal, stained with hematoxylin–eosin), and examined and images collected by light microscopy. Cell size in each section was calculated by the ImageJ software; the top 30 cells in size were counted, and the mean and variance in cell size was calculated, for each development period examined. Measurements were made at three different sites of each tissue, for three sections from each fruit.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Analysis of differentially expressed genes</h3><p>The high-quality RNA was separately extracted from large (B227) and small (B214) fruited accessions at three developmental stages (0, 10, and 20 DAP), with three biological replicates (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM5">10</a>). Libraries were constructed according to the protocol for the Illumina HiSeq4000 platform. All clean RNA-seq reads, generated from each sample, were mapped onto the assembly sequences, using TopHat2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Pertea, M. et al. Transcript-level expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with HISAT, StringTie and Ballgown. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1650–1667 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1974">36</a></sup> (version 2.1.0) with default parameters. The generated BAM format alignments, together with the gene GTF annotation file, were then fed to htseq-count (version 0.11.2) to compute the read counts. Differentially expressed gene (DEG) analysis was performed using the edgeR package implemented in R<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Robinson, M. D., McCarthy, D. J. &amp; Smyth, G. K. edgeR: a Bioconductor package for differential expression analysis of digital gene expression data. Bioinformatics 26, 139–140 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1978">62</a></sup>. DEGs were identified using a false discovery rate set at &lt;0.05 and fold change &gt;2 as cutoffs. The FPKM (fragments per kilobase of exon model per million reads mapped) values were computed using stringtie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Pertea, M. et al. Transcript-level expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with HISAT, StringTie and Ballgown. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1650–1667 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d17209e1982">36</a></sup> (v1.3.4d).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Immunoblotting</h3><p>The agrobacterium GV3101 cells harboring the pSuper-1300-35S-SAUR and vectors were inoculated in LB culture medium and cultured overnight at 28 °C. The agrobacterium culture was centrifuged and suspended with IM (inoculation medium: 10 mM MES (2-(<i>N</i>-morpholino)ethanesulfonic acid), 10 mM MgCl<sub>2</sub>, 500 µM AS (acetosyringone)). After 2 h incubation, at room temperature in darkness, the agrobacterium culture was centrifuged and suspended again with IM, and the final concentration of agrobacterium (measured by OD<sub>600</sub>) was adjusted to 0.3, 0.6, and 0.9. The agrobacterium inoculum was infiltrated into healthy and fully expanded cotyledons of 1-week-old wax gourd plants using a needleless syringe. Total protein was extracted at 3 dpi and mouse anti-MYC (ABmart, Lot number: 294166) antibody was used at 1:3000 concentration for immunoblots. The cell size of cotyledonary epidermal pavement cells was investigated, at 5 dpi, on free-hand sections. Cell size in each section was calculated by the ImageJ software.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">GWAS of fruit-size-related traits</h3><p>Fruit-size-related traits, including weight, length, diameter, and thickness, were evaluated three times during the spring of 2014, 2015, and 2016, at the Guangdong Academy of Agricultural Sciences (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM5">11</a>). Since the phenotypic data across years were found to be highly consistent, the average values for each trait were used for further analysis. GWAS were performed using Emmax<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Kang, H. M. et al. Variance component model to account for sample structure in genome-wide association studies. Nat. Genet 42, 348–354 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d17209e2012">63</a></sup>. A total of 2,237,614 SNPs, with a missing rate ≤10% and MAF ≥5% were used, and the average values of fruit-size-related traits were utilized for the association study. GWAS threshold was set using <i>N</i> (the effective number of independent SNPs, <i>p</i> = 1/<i>N</i>). The effective number of independent SNPs was calculated using Genetic type 1 Error Calculator (GEC) software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Li, M. X., Yeung, J. M., Cherny, S. S. &amp; Sham, P. C. Evaluating the effective numbers of independent tests and significant p-value thresholds in commercial genotyping arrays and public imputation reference datasets. Hum. Genet 131, 747–756 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d17209e2026">64</a></sup>. Finally, the signals with <i>p</i> &lt; 10<sup>−6</sup> were considered as the significantly associated sites.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its Supplementary Information files. A reporting summary for this Article is available as a Supplementary Information file. The datasets generated and analyzed during the current study are available from the corresponding author upon request. The wax gourd genome sequences have been deposited in GenBank of NCBI with BioProject ID PRJNA430006 [<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA430006">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA430006</a>]. The raw sequenced reads from the 146 wax gourd accessions have been deposited in the Genome Sequence Archive of the BIG Data Center under accession number <a href="https://bigd.big.ac.cn/search?dbId=gsa&amp;q=CRA001259">CRA001259</a> and in the sequence read archive (SRA) of NCBI under accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRP224893">SRP224893</a>. The raw transcriptome sequences have been deposited in the Genome Sequence Archive of the BIG Data Center under accession number <a href="https://bigd.big.ac.cn/search?dbId=gsa&amp;q=CRA001814">CRA001814</a> and in the SRA of NCBI under accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRP224600">SRP224600</a>. The source data underlying Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM6">1</a>a, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM6">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM6">4</a>b, c, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM6">5a, b, d</a>, as well as Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">8</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13185-3#MOESM1">11</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p>

Bennett, JB. Smith, JS. Heslop-Harrison, " /><meta itemprop="datePublished" content="1982" /><meta itemprop="headline" content="Bennett, M. D., Smith, J. B. &amp; Heslop-Harrison, J. S.
